SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
Our borings generally encountered a pavement structure consisting of about 
4 to 9 inches of asphaltic concrete overlying medium dense sandy gravel and silty sand 
fill material to depths of about 1 to 4 feet below the existing ground surface. The fill was 
generally underlain by alluvium, and/or estuarine and swamp deposits to the maximum 
depth drilled.  In general, alluvium consisting of stiff clayey silts was encountered to a 
depth of about 12 feet below the existing ground surface in the shallow borings drilled.  
For the deeper borings drilled in the vicinity of bridge/culvert crossing areas, estuarine 
and swamp deposits consisting of soft to very soft sandy silts, silty clays and clayey silts 
underlain by alluvium consisting of loose to medium dense sandy gravel and medium stiff 
clayey silts were encountered to the maximum drilled depth of 42.5 feet below existing 
ground surface. 
Groundwater was encountered at depths of approximately 3.5 to 11.5 feet below 
existing grades at the time of our field exploration. The groundwater levels encountered 
generally correspond to about elevations +18.7 to +36.8 feet MSL. It is anticipated the 
groundwater depth will vary based upon tides, rainfall, surface water runoff, seasonal 
precipitation and other factors. 
We anticipate that the majority of the water main inverts will be on the order of about 
4 to 5 feet below the existing pavement surface. However, the pipe invert will be extended 
to depths of about 10 to 15 feet below the pavement surface where the new water main 
traverses under the existing bridge or box culvert at four-stream crossing areas.  Installation 
of the proposed water mains will involve excavation into the underlying fills, alluvium, 
estuarine and swamp deposits. In general, we anticipate that the in-situ soils along the 
proposed water main alignments may be excavated with normal heavy excavation 
equipment. 
Where deep excavations (greater than 5 feet in depth) are planned, temporary 
shoring or sloping and benching should be implemented for the trench excavations. Based 
on our site observations, we believe that temporary cut slopes for open-cut excavations are 
not practical. In this case, trench walls would have to be cut vertically necessitating the use 
of shoring during construction. One possible method of shoring would be to use interlocking 
sheet pile shoring. The use of a sheet pile shoring system may also serve as a cut-off wall 
to aid in the dewatering operations.  
Dewatering of excavations will be necessary where the existing groundwater level is 
above the bottom of the proposed excavation. Dewatering of the excavation may involve 
SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page iv 
Hawaii • California 
moderate to large volumes of water due to the granular nature of the on-site soils. Based on 
the results of our field permeability tests, we estimate that the subsurface permeability of the 
on-site soils may be on the order of about 6.6 x 10-3 to 1.8 x 10-4 centimeters per second.  
In general, the near-surface sandy and gravelly soils encountered during our field 
exploration may be used in the upper portion of the trench backfill from a level 12 inches 
above the pipes to the pavement subgrade provided that the materials are free of vegetation, 
deleterious materials, organic material, and clay lumps and rock fragments greater than 
3 inches in maximum dimension. 
The text of this report should be referred to for detailed discussion and specific 
recommendations for the installation of the new water lines.

SECTION 1.  GENERAL 
1.1 
Introduction 
This report presents the results of our geotechnical engineering exploration 
performed for the Ahilama Road Water Main and Ahuimanu Water System Improvements 
project in the Kaneohe area on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The project location and 
general vicinity are shown on the Project Location Map, Plate 1. 
This report summarizes the findings and presents our geotechnical engineering 
recommendations derived from our field exploration, laboratory testing, and engineering 
analyses for the water system improvement project. These recommendations are 
intended for the design of trench excavation, dewatering, backfilling, thrust block/vault 
design, pavement restoration, and corrosion potential only. The findings and 
recommendations presented herein are subject to the limitations noted at the end of this 
report. 
1.2 
Project Considerations 
The project involves improvements to the existing water supply system along 
several streets in the Kaneohe area on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The planned 
improvements will involve the installation of water mains, fire hydrants, service laterals, 
pipe locator devices along waterlines, thrust blocks, concrete valve vault and other 
appurtenances.  
Based on the information provided, we understand that the proposed improvements 
project, encompassing three existing systems: Waihee 265’, Windward 500’ and Windward 
272’, includes installation of the new 8-inch and 12-inch water mains and abandonment of 
the existing water system.  The new water mains include the following: 
Ahilama Road: 2,300 Linear Feet (LF) of 12-inch water main
Mapele Road: 1,200 LF of 12-inch water main
Mapele Place: 950 LF of 8-inch water main
Ahaolelo Road: 675 LF of 8-inch water main

Since dewatering will be required to construct portions of the new lines, deeper 
borings and conversion into monitoring wells were performed to obtain groundwater 
samples for water quality testing for National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System 
(NPDES) dewatering permit application purposes. 
The project also requires geotechnical exploration for evaluation of various waterline 
crossing alternatives, including open-cut, trenchless, hanging pipeline on the existing bridge 
and hanging pipeline on a new pipeline bridge structure, for the construction of the new water 
mains at one bridge and three boxes and/or pipe culvert locations.  Geotechnical 
consideration and evaluation of various water line crossing alternatives were provided to the 
prime consultant and presented in a separate evaluation report.  Reference to the subject 
evaluation report should be made for detailed discussion and recommendations on the 
selected waterline crossing alternative.     
1.3 
Scope of Work 
The purpose of our geotechnical exploration was to obtain an overview of the 
subsurface conditions to develop an idealized soil/rock data set to formulate geotechnical 
engineering recommendations for the proposed water system improvements. Our work 
was performed in general accordance with our revised fee proposal, dated June 7, 2018, 
and Task Order No. 109191, issued under the Master Consulting Services Subcontract 
dated January 1, 2015. Our scope of work generally consisted of the following tasks and 
work efforts. 
1.
Review of available in-house soil and geologic information for areas
around the water main project location.
2.
Filing of application for excavation and street-usage permits from the City and
County of Honolulu prior to drilling crew mobilization.
3.
Staking out and coordination of utility toning with various utility companies and
clearance of the boring locations by our field engineer/geologist.
4.
Traffic control at the boring locations during our field exploration and
abandonment of the monitoring wells.
5.
Mobilization and demobilization of a truck-mounted drill rig, water truck, and
two operators to and from the project site.

SECTION 2.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
2.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu was built by the extrusion of basaltic lavas from the Waianae 
and Koolau shield volcanoes.  The older Waianae Volcano is estimated to be middle to 
late Pliocene in age and forms the bulk of the western third of the island.  The younger 
Koolau Volcano is estimated to be late Pliocene to early Pleistocene (Ice Age) in age and 
forms the majority of the eastern two-thirds of the island.  Waianae became extinct while 
Koolau was still active, and its eastern flank was partially buried below Koolau lavas 
banking against its eastern flank.  These banked or ponded lavas formed a broad plateau 
referred to as the Schofield Plateau. 
In the mountainous regions of Hawaii, the erosion processes are dominated by 
detachment of soil and rock masses from the valley walls, which are then transported 
downslope toward the axis of a valley primarily by gravity as colluvium.  Once these 
materials reach the stream in the central portion of a valley, alluvial processes become 
dominant, and the sediments are transported and deposited as alluvium.  In general, stream 
flows in Hawaii are intermittent and flashy, such that the stream flows transmit large volumes 
of water for very short durations.  Because of this, transport of sediments is intermittent, and 
the bulk of the stream's hydraulic load consists of a poorly sorted mixture of boulders, 
cobbles, gravel, sands, and fines.  When the erosion base levels change, these sediment 
loads are left as deposits. 
When deposits are left in-place for long periods of time, chemical processes begin 
to alter the materials, simultaneously causing a breakdown or weathering of the material. 
Chemical processes also cause induration, or cementation, of the coarse-grained portion 
of the sediment into a poorly-consolidated sedimentary rock or conglomerate. 
Simultaneously, erosion continues in the areas above the valley floors and upstream in 
headwaters.  This continued erosion generates material which is transported downslope 
covering the older alluvial deposits.  Depending on the local base level and rate of 
transport, these new sediments are generally transient in terms of geologic time. 
In addition, their consistency and density are generally less than those of older, partially 
consolidated deposits. 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 5 
Hawaii • California 
The project site is situated near the base of the Koolau Pali, a high cliff that extends 
along most of the windward side of the Koolau Mountain Range on the Island of Oahu. 
The climate along the Pali is typified by a high to very high average annual rainfall. 
Following the main shield building stage of the Koolau volcano in the late Pliocene to 
early Pleistocene Epoch, stream erosion, landslide and deep weathering of the basalt 
rock became the dominant agents affecting the morphology of the area. 
The project site is generally composed of alluvial soils derived from large-scale 
erosion and landslide along the base of the Koolau forming colluvial aprons that were 
subsequently transported by runoff to create the alluvial fans generally forming the 
present topography of the region. 
2.2 
Existing Site Conditions 
The project site is in the Kaneohe area on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. It is planned 
to install water line mains, fire hydrants, service laterals, pipe locator devices along 
waterlines, thrust blocks, concrete valve vault and other appurtenances along existing 
Ahilama Street, Mapele Road, Mapele Place and Ahaolelo Road.  Both Kahaluu 
Elementary School and Kahaluu Fire Station are located at the project limit of Ahilama 
Street near the intersection with Waihee Road.  The existing roadways are paved with 
asphaltic concrete with no shoulder areas.  There are abundant vegetation and trees 
along both sides of the roadways.  The top of pavement elevations vary from about +26 
to +135 feet Mean Sea Level (MSL).  With reference to the City and County of Honolulu’s 
Public GIS Website, the project site is in an area generally classified as AG-2, General 
Agriculture zone. 
2.3 
Subsurface Conditions 
Our field exploration consisted of drilling and sampling twenty-one borings, 
designated as Boring Nos. 1 through 21, extending to depths of about 11.5 to 42.5 feet 
below the existing ground surface. Continuous penetration with a blunt tip was performed 
at Boring Nos. 5, 13 and 15 to depths of about 39 to 47.5 feet below the existing ground 
surface.  The approximate boring locations are shown on the General Site Plan, Plate 2 
and Site Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.8.

SECTION 3.  DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
Our borings generally encountered a pavement structure consisting of about 
4 to 9 inches of asphaltic concrete overlying a thin fill layer consisting of medium dense 
sandy gravel and silty sand fill material to depths of about 1 to 4 feet below the existing 
ground surface. The fill was generally underlain by estuarine deposits, swamp deposits 
and/or alluvium to the maximum depth drilled.  In general, alluvium consisting of stiff 
clayey silts was encountered to a depth of about 12 feet below the existing ground surface 
in the shallow borings drilled.  For the deeper borings drilled at bridge/culvert crossing 
areas, estuarine and swamp deposits consisting of soft to very soft sandy silts, silty clays 
and clayey silts underlain by alluvium consisting of loose to medium dense sandy gravel 
and medium stiff clayey silts were encountered to the maximum drilled depth of 42.5 feet 
below existing ground surface.  Groundwater was encountered at depths of approximately 
3.5 to 11.5 feet below existing grades at the time of our field exploration. 
We anticipate that the majority of the water main inverts will be on the order of 
about 4 to 5 feet below the existing pavement surface. However, the pipe invert will be 
extended to depths of about 10 to 15 feet below the pavement surface where the new 
water main traverses under an existing line or box culvert.  Installation of the proposed 
water mains will involve excavation into the underlying fills, alluvium, estuarine and 
swamp deposits. In general, we anticipate that the in-situ soils along the proposed water 
main alignments may be excavated with normal heavy excavation equipment. 
Where deep excavations (greater than 5 feet in depth) are planned, temporary 
shoring or sloping and benching should be implemented for the trench excavations. 
Based on our site observations, we believe that temporary cut slopes for open-cut 
excavations are not practical. In this case, trench walls would have to be cut vertically 
necessitating the use of shoring during construction. One possible method of shoring 
would be to use interlocking sheet pile shoring. The use of a sheet pile shoring system 
may also serve as a cut-off wall to aid in the dewatering operations.  
Dewatering of excavations will be necessary where the existing groundwater level 
is above the bottom of the proposed excavation. Dewatering of the excavation may

involve moderate to large volumes of water. Based on the results of our field permeability 
tests, we estimate that the subsurface permeability of the on-site soils may be on the 
order of about 6.6 x 10-3 to 1.8 x 10-4 centimeters per second.  
In general, the near-surface sandy and gravelly soils encountered during our field

above the pipes to the pavement subgrade provided that the materials are free of

Detailed discussions for design of the water system improvement project including 
trench excavation, trench excavation support, dewatering, backfilling, thrust block/vault 
design, pavement restoration, corrosion protection and other geotechnical aspects of the 
project are presented in the following sections. 
3.1 
Trench Excavation 
We anticipate that majority of the pipe inverts will be on the order of about 4 to 5 
feet below the existing pavement surface. However, the pipe invert will be extended to 
depths of about 10 to 15 feet below the pavement surface where the new water main 
traverses under an existing utility line or box culvert.  Installation of the proposed water 
mains will involve excavation into the underlying fills, alluvium, estuarine and swamp 
deposits. In general, we anticipate that the in-situ soils along the proposed water main 
alignments may be excavated with normal heavy excavation equipment. 
Excavated soils should not be stockpiled closer than a horizontal distance equal 
to the depth of the excavation from the edge of the excavation to reduce the potential for 
excessive ground movement. In addition, the excavated soils should be stockpiled at least 
15 feet away from existing underground utilities to reduce the potential for appreciable 
ground movement or subsidence, which may damage the existing underground utility 
lines. 
The above discussion regarding the excavation of the subsurface materials is 
based on field data obtained from the borings drilled at the project site. We recommend 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 9

encouraging contractors bidding on this project to examine the site conditions and soil 
data to make their own interpretation. 
3.2 
Trench Excavation Support 
We anticipate excavation depths of up to about 4 to 5 feet below the pavement 
surface will be required for the installation of the new water mains. Deeper excavations 
up to 15 feet below the pavement surface may be required where the water main 
traverses under an existing utility or box culvert. Where deeper excavations (greater than 
5 feet in depth) are planned, temporary shoring or sloping and benching should be 
implemented for the trench excavations. Based on our site observations, we believe that 
temporary cut slopes for open-cut excavations are not practical. In this case, trench walls 
would have to be cut vertically necessitating the use of shoring during construction. 
One possible method of shoring would be to use interlocking sheet pile shoring. 
The use of a sheet pile shoring system may also serve as a cut-off wall to aid in the 
dewatering operations, which is further discussed in the “Dewatering” section herein. The 
sheet piles should be driven with a suitable hammer to a sufficient depth to reduce the 
potential for areal ground subsidence and to reduce the amount of dewatering within the 
excavations due to the relatively permeable nature of the subsoils. It should be noted that 
the excavations will likely encounter very soft/loose soil deposits at the bottom of the 
excavation. Therefore, there is potential for bottom heave in these very soft/loose soil 
conditions. The contractor should carefully evaluate the potential for bottom heave and 
design the shoring system accordingly. 
It is important to install adequate sheeting prior to the excavation and to maintain 
it tightly against the excavation walls with proper bracing during excavation. The properly 
braced sheeting is essential to reduce appreciable lateral movements of the adjacent 
ground into the excavation, which may result in potential settlements or distress to 
adjacent structures or other improvements, such as roads or utilities. 
The excavation support and shoring system used must comply with applicable 
safety requirements. The contractor should be solely responsible for trench excavation 
and the adequacy and safety of the shoring installation. The contractor’s representative, 
who should be required to continuously present on-site during excavation and 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 10 
Hawaii • California 
construction works, will have the best opportunity to promptly observe changing 
conditions during construction, such as unforeseen subsoils, unexpectedly high 
groundwater table, inappropriate construction sequence or techniques, etc., which may 
affect shoring stability. 
However, it must be noted that some minor movements of the shoring system and 
the adjacent ground may still occur due to changes in earth stresses during excavation. 
Due to the complexity of the stress changes, it is difficult to accurately estimate the 
magnitude of movement. The magnitude also depends greatly upon workmanship, such 
as how quickly and tightly the shoring and bracing supports are installed, the subsoil 
conditions, the size of the excavation, and the rate of excavation. In addition, it should be 
noted that settlement of the existing ground may occur as a result of the vibrations 
generated during the extraction of the sheet pile shoring. Therefore, the contractor should 
give special attention during the sheet pile removal process to reduce the potential for 
appreciable ground settlement. 
Therefore, it is important to realize that the excavation shoring should be installed 
properly and as early as practical, if necessary, and that the adjacent ground should be 
continuously monitored for cracks, dips and/or other indications of movements with 
instruments. 
3.3 
Pipe Cushion 
The stress distribution against the bottom of a pipe has a significant effect on the 
load supporting capacity of the pipe. Therefore, the pipe cushion is an important design 
consideration. For pipes bearing on the medium dense silty sand and sandy gravel 
surface fill soils, a minimum of 6 inches of compacted granular cushion material, such as 
No. 3B Fine gravel (ASTM C33, No. 67 Gradation) should be provided below the pipe. 
Where soft and/or loose soils are encountered at or near the invert of the pipes, such as 
in the estuarine/swamp deposits, a stabilization layer consisting of an additional 24 inches 
of open-graded gravel, such as No. 3B Fine gravel, wrapped in a non-woven filter fabric 
(Mirafi 180N or equivalent) should be provided below the cushion layer for uniform 
support.  A typical trench detail is provided on Plate 4. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 11 
Hawaii • California 
The granular cushion layer should extend beyond the pipe sides a minimum width 
of one-fourth (1/4) the outside pipe diameter, or trench width, whichever is greater, and 
should extend 12 inches above the pipe. Before the placement of cushion material, a 
Geolabs representative should observe the excavated trench bottom to confirm that firm 
materials are exposed at the bottom of the trench or whether a stabilization layer should 
be provided below the cushion layer. 
3.4 
Trench Backfill 
It should be noted that loads on buried pipes are influenced by the width of the 
trench, the unit weight of trench backfill material and the friction resistance between the 
backfill material and the trench walls.  To calculate the vertical loads on the buried pipes, 
we recommend that an average unit weight of 120 pounds per cubic feet (pcf) for the 
trench backfill material and a coefficient of friction of 0.3 could be used.  Earth forces 
acting upon the pipe generally increase rapidly with the width of the trench.  Therefore, 
the width of the trench should be kept to a minimum.  Traffic loads on the buried pipes 
should also be considered for the portion of the pipes located in roadway areas. 
In general, the trench backfill should be placed in accordance with the Water 
System Standards, State of Hawaii 2002. The first zone of backfill extending from the 
cushion material to at least 12 inches above the top of the pipes should consist of select 
free-draining granular material (ASTM C33, No. 67 Gradation) to reduce the compaction 
effort required and resulting stresses on the pipe. In addition, it is critical to use this 
free-draining material to reduce the potential for formation of voids below the haunches 
of pipes and to provide adequate support for the sides of the pipes. Improper trench 
backfill could result in backfill settlement and pipe damage. 
The upper portion of the trench backfill from a level 12 inches above the pipes to 
the pavement subgrade should consist of the excavated on-site surface fill soils or 
imported fill materials that are free of vegetation, deleterious materials, organic material, 
and clay lumps and rock fragments greater than 3 inches in maximum dimension. It 
should be noted that the fill requirements presented herein are intended as guidelines 
only and may be modified based on Geolabs additional laboratory testing and field 
observations on the available fill materials. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 12 
Hawaii • California 
Imported fill materials used for trench backfill should be well graded from coarse 
to fine with particles no larger than 3 inches in largest dimension. The material should 
contain between 10 and 30 percent particles passing the No. 200 sieve. The material 
should have a laboratory California Bearing Ratio (CBR) value of 20 or higher and a swell 
potential of 1 percent or less when tested in accordance with ASTM D1883. Geolabs 
should test the imported fill materials for conformance with these recommendations. 
As an alternative, to facilitate the backfilling and reduce the time required for 
applying compaction effort, Lightweight Cellular Concrete (LWCC) may be used to backfill 
the trenches. The LWCC shall be Class III and have an in-place density between 65 and 
85 pcf, and strength between 150 and 250 pounds per square inch (psi). The use of 
LWCC would also reduce the potential for future settlement of the trench backfill under 
the repeated wheel loading from traffic on the roadway. 
3.5 
Trench Backfill Compaction and Placement Requirements 
Backfill materials consisting of the on-site surface fill soils should be 
moisture-conditioned to at least 2 percent above the optimum moisture, placed in level 
lifts not exceeding 8 inches in loose thickness, and compacted to a minimum of 
90 percent relative compaction. The upper 3 feet below the pavement grade should be 
compacted to a minimum of 95 percent relative compaction. 
Imported fill materials should be moisture-conditioned to above the optimum 
moisture, placed in level lifts of about 8 inches in loose thickness, and compacted to a 
minimum of 90 percent relative compaction. Aggregate base course and subbase 
materials should be moisture-conditioned to above the optimum moisture content, placed 
in level lifts not exceeding 8 inches in loose thickness, and compacted to a minimum of 
95 percent relative compaction. Relative compaction refers to the in-place dry density of 
soil expressed as a percentage of the maximum dry density of the same soil established 
in accordance with ASTM D1557. Optimum moisture is the water content (percentage by 
dry weight) corresponding to the maximum dry density. 
Compaction should be accomplished by sheepsfoot rollers, vibratory rollers, or 
other types of acceptable compaction equipment. Water tamping, jetting, or ponding 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 13 
Hawaii • California 
should not be allowed to compact the excavated surface on-site fill soils or imported fill 
materials. 
Geolabs should perform observations and soil density tests during backfilling to 
assist the contractor in obtaining the required degree of compaction and the proper 
moisture content.  
3.6 
Valve Vault Foundations 
We envision that concrete valve structures may be required for the proposed 
waterline. Based on the subsurface conditions encountered at the site, we recommend 
an allowable bearing pressure of up to 1,500 pounds per square foot (psf). This bearing 
value is for dead-plus-live loads and may be increased by one-third (1/3) for transient 
loads, such as those caused by seismic forces. 
If soft and/or loose materials are encountered at the bottom of the valve structure 
excavations, a stabilization layer consisting of an additional 24 inches of open-graded 
gravel wrapped in a non-woven filter fabric (Mirafi 180N or equivalent) should be provided 
below the bedding layer for uniform support. 
If foundations are designed and constructed in accordance with our 
recommendations, we estimate the total settlements of the valve vault structure to be less 
than 1 inch. 
Lateral loads acting on the valve vault structure may be resisted by friction between 
the base of the foundation and on-site soils and by passive earth pressure developed 
against the near-vertical faces of the embedded of valve vault.  A coefficient of friction of 
0.30 may be used for foundations bearing on the compacted on-site soils.  Resistance to 
lateral loads due to passive pressure may be estimated using an equivalent fluid pressure 
of 250 pounds per square foot per foot of depth (pcf).  Unless covered by pavements or 
slabs, the passive resistance in the upper 12 inches below finished grade should be 
neglected. 
The valve vault structure should be designed to resist the lateral earth pressure 
due to the adjacent soils and surcharge effects.  The following lateral earth pressure, 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 14 
Hawaii • California 
expressed in equivalent fluid pressures of pounds per square foot per foot of depth (pcf), 
may be used in the design of the valve vault walls.

Non-expansive, select granular fill materials should be used to backfill behind the 
valve vault structures.  It is assumed that the backfill behind retaining structures will be 
compacted to between 90 and 95 percent relative compaction. Over-compaction of the 
retaining structure backfill should be avoided. 
Surcharge stresses due to areal surcharges, traffic loads, line loads, and point 
loads within a horizontal distance equal to the depth of the wall should be considered in 
the design. For uniform surcharge stresses imposed on the loaded side of the wall, a 
rectangular distribution with uniform pressure equal to 50 percent of the vertical surcharge 
pressure acting on the entire height of the wall should be used. Additional analyses during 
design may be needed to evaluate the surcharge effects of point loads and line loads. 
3.7 
Thrust Blocks 
We envision that thrust blocks will be required at some locations to provide 
resistance to the thrust forces due to the pressurized pipes. The thrust force acting on the 
pipes may be resisted by passive earth pressure acting against the face of the thrust 
block. 
We anticipate that the majority of the planned inverts would be about 4 to 5 feet 
below the existing ground surface. However, some of the thrust blocks may need to be 
located at deeper depths of about 10 to 15 feet below the pavement surface in areas 
where the new water main traverses the existing utility or underground box culvert. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 15 
Hawaii • California 
Based on our field exploration, variable materials ranging from alluvium consisting 
of medium stiff to hard silts and clays to estuarine/swamp deposits consisting of soft to 
very soft sandy silts to silty clays were encountered within the depths explored. We 
recommend using lateral bearing pressures of up to 1,000 and 500 pounds per square 
foot (psf) for the design of thrust blocks bearing on the medium stiff clayey soils and soft 
silty clayey soils, respectively. 
3.8 
Settlements 
The primary settlement of the water mains is normally caused by the difference in 
the unit weight of the lighter excavated original earth and the heavier compacted backfill 
material placed over the pipes. The net increase in loading will cause settlement of the 
underlying subsoils below the trench invert. Based on our engineering analyses, 
settlement on the order of less than 1 inch is anticipated for the project. 
The above estimate assumes that proper construction procedures and good 
workmanship will be engaged during construction. Additional settlement could occur if 
improper trench support is used. 
3.9 
Dewatering 
Dewatering of excavations will be necessary where the existing groundwater level 
is above the bottom of the proposed excavation. Groundwater was encountered at depths 
ranging from about 3.5 to 11.5 feet below the existing ground surface in the borings drilled 
during our field exploration. Based on the groundwater levels anticipated and the 
permeable nature of the sandy soils to be encountered during the excavations, 
dewatering will be required at the planned excavations of the project construction. 
Therefore, dewatering provisions will need to be included in the contract documents for 
the proposed construction. Since the excavation will involve the discharge of 
groundwater, an NPDES permit will be required for this discharge. Groundwater sampling 
and testing were performed for the project in support of the NPDES permit application 
and the results are presented in Appendix D. 
It is anticipated that the granular soils encountered are relatively permeable. 
Therefore, dewatering of the excavation may involve moderate to large volumes of water. 
Dewatering by means of a well point system along the outside of the excavations is not 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 16 
Hawaii • California 
recommended. The resultant areal depression of the natural groundwater table could 
induce consolidation of the compressible subsoils resulting in potential ground 
settlements, which could affect the existing structures and utility lines. The potential 
impact of the dewatering system selected on depressing the natural groundwater table 
must be carefully evaluated by the contractor prior to dewatering. 
It is our opinion that a cut-off wall system, such as interlocking sheet piles, should 
be considered to aid in dewatering the excavation. However, sumps will be needed to 
collect water that percolates up into the base of the excavation or infiltrates through the 
sheet piles. The sheet piles should be driven to an adequate depth to reduce the potential 
for areal ground subsidence and to reduce the amount of dewatering within the 
excavations in areas underlain by granular subsoils with generally high permeability. The 
use of an interlocking sheet pile shoring support system with a tremie concrete plug is 
relatively watertight, which should allow the groundwater levels outside the excavations 
to be maintained close to the original pre-construction levels. Therefore, some type of 
groundwater control requirement should be specified in the contract documents. 
The contractor is responsible for dewatering for construction. The contractor 
should select equipment and methods of dewatering, and he/she should be aware that 
modifications to the dewatering system may be required during construction depending 
on the conditions encountered. The dewatering method selected should have minimal 
impact on the groundwater level surrounding the proposed excavation. The dewatering 
operations should be coordinated with the trench shoring support such that the stability 
of the excavations is not jeopardized. The operations should be carried-out without 
softening the bottom of the excavations. 
It is our opinion that the definition of "Dewatering" in the contract documents should 
be written to include all works or systems required to lower the natural groundwater table 
and/or to exclude water from the excavations to allow construction of the proposed water 
mains under safe and dry conditions. These works or systems may include, but are not 
limited to, pumping, grouting, cut-off walls, cofferdams, tremie plugs to resist buoyant 
forces or any combination of the above or other possible methods. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 17 
Hawaii • California 
Subsurface Soil Permeability 
One constant head permeability test was conducted at the project site in Boring No. 
4 at a depth of about 11.5 feet below the existing ground surface.  Two falling head 
permeability tests were conducted at the project site, one in Boring No. 10 and one 
in Boring No. 12, at a depth of about 21.5 feet below the existing ground surface. 
Based on the results of our field permeability tests, we estimate that the subsurface 
permeability of the on-site soils may be on the order of about 6.6x10-3 to 1.8x10-4 
centimeters per second. Results of the field permeability tests are presented in 
Appendix C. 
It is our opinion that the above-estimated permeability values should be used with 
caution due to the normally heterogeneous nature of the alluvium and 
estuarine/swamp deposits. The actual subsurface soil permeability may range 
broadly and may also vary locally in terms of the orders of magnitude. Therefore, the 
contractor should give special attention to the site-specific dewatering plan for the 
proposed project. 
 Dewatering Considerations 
We suggest that the following three basic criteria be considered in the selection of a 
suitable method of dewatering: 
a.
The dewatering method should result in the least disturbance or
damage to existing buildings, roads, and environment.
b.
The dewatering method should maintain stability to provide safe and
dry working conditions for the excavation.
c.
The dewatering method should be sufficiently flexible to allow
modifications to accommodate various ground conditions.
Dewatering Precaution and Monitoring 
The contractor must carefully evaluate the potential impact of the dewatering system 
selected on depressing the natural groundwater table prior to dewatering. The 
contractor should retain a qualified geotechnical engineer to design and evaluate the 
dewatering system used. 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 18 
Hawaii • California 
The contractor should be solely responsible for the impact and safety of the 
dewatering operations. The contractor’s qualified representative, who should be 
continuously present on-site during dewatering activities, will have the best 
opportunity to promptly observe the effects of dewatering during construction and to 
implement, as soon as possible, necessary precautionary or remedial measures 
including, but not limited to, slowing down or stopping the dewatering operations. 
Where encountered at the bottom of excavations, permeable granular subsoils may 
be susceptible to piping and "quick" conditions. The dewatering operations should be 
carried-out without creating a "quick" condition or softening at the excavation 
bottoms. Therefore, the project dewatering operations should be performed without 
pumping out soil fines (pumping clear water only) and should be coordinated with the 
shoring installation such that the excavation stability is not adversely affected. 
Excessive pumping, which removes soil fines, may result in "blowing" or heaving of 
the excavation bottom or sides. 
Groundwater drawdown outside the excavation will cause additional settlement 
resulting from consolidation of the soft/loose compressible soils. Therefore, the use 
of a deep well system outside the excavations to draw down the groundwater level 
should not be allowed. 
Special caution should also be taken to avoid dewatering excavations connected to 
existing utility trenches.  If this occurs, the granular bedding and/or backfill in the 
existing utility trenches could act as subdrains and cause significant areal 
groundwater drawdown. Significant areal groundwater drawdown would result in 
ground settlements and potential damage to utility lines and/or other existing adjacent 
structures. 
For underground construction in a developed area, we believe that the importance of 
construction monitoring, including field observation and engineering supervision, 
should be emphasized. The extent of construction monitoring may vary with 
individual contractors. However, as a minimum, a damage survey of existing 
buildings and structures within 100 feet of the proposed excavation and dewatering 
site should be conducted by the contractor, including photographs and crack surveys, 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 19 
Hawaii • California 
prior to the commencement of construction.  Instrumentations such as settlement 
points and inclinometers should be considered, as deemed necessary. 
3.10 Groundwater Quality Testing 
Groundwater sampling and testing were performed in support of the NPDES 
dewatering permit application.  Groundwater monitoring wells were installed to depths of 
about 21.5 feet below the existing ground surface at the Boring Nos. 5, 10, and 12 locations. 
The installed groundwater monitoring well consists of a 2-inch diameter Schedule 40 PVC 
pipe installed to a depth of about 20 feet below the existing ground surface. The well 
consists of about 15 feet of well screen with 0.020-inch slot openings and about 4 feet of 
solid casing.  The surface completion consisted of a 6-inch well cover that was installed 
flush with the existing ground surface.  Groundwater monitoring, utilizing a vibrating wire 
piezometer and an electronic datalogger, was performed for about 4 weeks.  Ranges of 
groundwater levels encountered during our field exploration and groundwater monitoring 
are summarized in the following table.  The monitoring wells were sealed with cement grout 
and abandoned properly after completion of groundwater monitoring.

3.11 Pavement Restoration 
It is anticipated that asphaltic concrete pavement will be used to repave the 
trenches along the water main alignments within the roadway. We have assumed that the 
pavement subgrade will consist of the on-site silty sand fill soils encountered near the 
existing pavement surface. In general, the new pavements along the waterline trenches 
should match the existing pavement sections or the following flexible pavement section 
may be used, whichever is greater. 
Flexible Pavements 
  2.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete 
  4.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete Base (92 Percent Max Theoretical Specific Gravity) 
 Match Existing Aggregate Subbase (95 Percent Relative Compaction) 
10.0-Inch Minimum Total Pavement Thickness Over Compacted Subgrade 
The above section is based on our field exploration and laboratory testing, which 
indicates the on-site surface fill soils had a relatively low laboratory CBR values of about 
3 to 10 when compacted at near optimum moisture content. The above minimum 
pavement section is based on the assumption that the subgrade soils can be dried to 
near optimum moisture content and the subgrade could be compacted to a minimum of 
95 percent relative compaction. However, if the subgrade soils cannot be dried sufficiently 
to obtain the 95 percent compaction, the upper 1 foot of subgrade soils should be 
removed and replaced with imported fill material conforming to the requirements 
presented herein. 
Asphalt concrete base (ACB) material should consist of asphalt-treated basalt 
aggregate, placed in a layer not to exceed 6 inches in compacted thickness, and 
compacted to no less than 92 percent of the maximum theoretical specific gravity 
determined in accordance with AASHTO T-209 or ASTM D2041. Asphalt concrete (AC) 
should consist of Mix #4 in accordance with the Standard Specifications for Public Works 
Construction (September 1986). Asphalt concrete base (ACB) and asphalt concrete (AC) 
material should be constructed in accordance with Sections 32 and 34 of the Standard 
Specifications for Public Works Construction (September 1986), respectively. 
The imported subbase materials should consist of crushed basalt aggregate 
compacted to no less than 95 percent relative compaction with a minimum laboratory 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 21 
Hawaii • California 
CBR value of 30. CBR and field density tests should be performed on the actual subgrade 
soils encountered during construction to confirm the adequacy of the above section. 
3.12 Corrosion Potential 
Twenty-one sets of laboratory corrosivity tests, including pH (ASTM G51), Minimum 
Resistivity (ASTM G57), Chloride Content (EPA 300.0), and Sulfate Content (EPA 300.0), 
were performed by our laboratory and TestAmerica Laboratories, Inc. to evaluate the 
corrosivity of the on-site soils on selected soil samples obtained from our field exploration. 
The test results are summarized on Plate B-23 of Appendix B.  
Resistivity is generally recognized as one of the most significant soil characteristics 
with regard to the corrosivity of the soil to buried metallic objects. In general, the lower the 
resistivity, the greater the potential for corrosion of the buried metallic structure will have. 
Conversely, the higher the resistivity, the less likely the soil will contribute to the corrosion 
of metallic objects.  Results of the resistivity testing indicate that the on-site soils have 
resistivity values of about 1,000 to 8,000 ohm-cm.  Therefore, the on-site near-surface soils 
may be considered very corrosive to moderately corrosive (Corrosion Rating of 2 to 4) based 
on the Board of Water Supply, City & County of Honolulu Water System External Corrosion 
Control Standards dated 1991.  
The method used to control the corrosion of underground concrete pipelines and 
structures is dependent on the pH and the chloride and sulfate contents found in the soil. 
Based on the chloride and sulfate content tests performed on the on-site soils, the tested 
chloride and sulfate content values are relatively low and may be considered 
“non-corrosive” to underground concrete pipelines and structures. However, the tested 
pH values range from 4.71 to 8.58.  and it should be noted that areas with the tested pH 
values of less than 5.0 shall be given a Corrosion Rating of 1, extremely corrosive, based 
on the Board of Water Supply, City & County of Honolulu Water System External 
Corrosion Control Standards dated 1991. It may be appropriate to consult with a 
professional corrosion engineer to review the test results and provide detailed 
recommendations for corrosion protection.

GEOLABS, INC.

3.13 Design Review 
Drawings and specifications for the proposed construction should be forwarded to 
Geolabs for review and written comments prior to bid advertisement. This review is 
necessary to evaluate the conformance of the plans and specifications with the intent of 
the earthwork recommendations provided herein. If this review is not made, Geolabs 
cannot assume responsibility for misinterpretation of the recommendations presented. 
3.14 Construction Observation 
We recommend retaining Geolabs for geotechnical engineering services during 
construction. The critical items of construction monitoring that require "Special Inspection" 
include periodic observation of the trench excavation and placement of bedding materials 
and trench backfill.  
A Geolabs representative should also monitor other aspects of earthwork 
construction to observe compliance with the design concepts, specifications, or 
recommendations and to expedite suggestions for design changes that may be required 
in the event that subsurface conditions differ from those anticipated at the time this report 
was prepared. Geolabs should be accorded the opportunity to provide geotechnical 
engineering services during construction to confirm our assumptions in providing the 
recommendations presented herein. 
If the actual exposed subsurface conditions encountered during construction differ 
from those assumed or considered in this report, Geolabs should be contacted to review 
and/or revise the geotechnical recommendations presented herein.

SECTION 4.  LIMITATIONS 
The analyses and recommendations submitted herein are based in part upon 
information obtained from the field borings and bulk samples. Variations of conditions 
between and beyond the field data points may occur, and the nature and extent of these 
variations may not become evident until construction is underway. If variations then 
appear evident, it will be necessary to re-evaluate the recommendations presented 
herein. 
The field boring locations are approximate, having been estimated by using a 
hand-held Global Positioning System (GPS) device, shown on the Google Earth Pro 
software and transposed onto the Conceptual Waterline Plan and Profile sheets 
transmitted by AECOM on September 18, 2019. Elevations of the borings were 
interpolated based on the spot elevations shown on the Waterline Plan and Profile sheets. 
The physical locations and elevations of the borings should be considered accurate only 
to the degree implied by the methods used. 
The stratification lines shown on the graphic representations of the borings depict 
the approximate boundaries between soil and/or rock types and, as such, may denote a 
gradual transition. Water level data from the borings were measured at the times shown 
on the graphic representations and/or presented in the text herein. These data have been 
reviewed and interpolations made in the formulation of this report. However, it must be 
noted that fluctuation may occur due to variation in rainfall, tides, temperature, and other 
factors. 
This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of AECOM for specific 
application to the proposed Ahilama Road Water Main and Ahuimanu Water System 
Improvements project as described herein in accordance with generally accepted 
geotechnical engineering principles and practices. No warranty is expressed or implied. 
The owner/client should be aware that unanticipated soil conditions are commonly 
encountered. Unforeseen subsurface conditions, such as perched groundwater, soft 
deposits, hard layers or cavities, may occur in localized areas and may require additional 
probing or corrections in the field (which may result in construction delays) to attain a 
SECTION 4. LIMITATIONS 
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 24 
Hawaii • California 
properly constructed project. Therefore, a sufficient contingency fund is recommended to 
accommodate these possible extra costs. 
This geotechnical engineering exploration conducted at the project site was not 
intended to investigate the potential presence of hazardous materials existing at the site. 
It should be noted that the equipment, techniques, and personnel used to conduct a 
geo-environmental exploration differ substantially from those applied in geotechnical 
engineering. 
END OF LIMITATIONS

AHUIMANU WATER SYSTEM IMPROVEMENTS
AHILAMA ROAD WATER MAIN AND
TYPICAL TRENCH DETAIL
KANEOHE, OAHU, HAWAII
APPENDIX A
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
DECEMBER 2019     Page A-1 
Hawaii • California 
A P P E N D I X   A 
Field Exploration 
We explored the subsurface conditions by drilling and sampling twenty-one borings, 
designated as Boring Nos. 1 through 21, to depths of about 11.5 to 42.5 feet below the 
existing ground surface at the approximate locations shown on the General Site Plan, Plate 
2 and Site Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.8. We used a truck-mounted drill rig equipped with 
continuous flight augers to advance the borings. 
Our geologists classified the materials encountered in the borings by visual and 
textural examination in the field in general accordance with ASTM D2488, Standard Practice 
for Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored the drilling operations on a 
near-continuous (full-time) basis. These classifications were further reviewed visually and 
by testing in the laboratory. Soils were classified in general accordance with ASTM D2487, 
Standard Practice for Classification of Soils for Engineering Purposes (Unified Soil 
Classification System), as shown on the Soil Log Legend, Plate A-0.1. Deviations made to 
the soil classification in accordance with ASTM D2487 are described in the Soil 
Classification Log Key, Plate A-0.2. Graphic representations of the materials encountered 
are presented on the Logs of Borings, Plates A-1 through A-21. 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil samples were obtained in general accordance with 
ASTM D3550-01(2007), Ring-Lined Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 3-inch 
OD Modified California sampler with a 140-pound hammer falling 30 inches. In addition, 
some samples were obtained from the borings in general accordance with ASTM D1586, 
Penetration Test and Split-Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 2-inch OD standard 
penetration sampler using the same hammer and drop. The blow counts needed to drive 
the sampler the second and third 6 inches of an 18-inch drive are shown as the 
“Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Boring at the appropriate sample depths. The 
penetration resistance shown on the Logs of Borings indicates the number of blows 
required for the specific sampler type used.  The blow counts may need to be factored to 
obtain the Standard Penetration Test (SPT) blow counts.  
Pocket penetrometer tests were performed on selected cohesive soil samples 
retrieved in the field. The pocket penetrometer test provides an indication of the 
unconfined compressive strength of the sample. Pocket penetrometer test results are 
summarized on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths.

COHESIVE SOIL (- #200    50%)
PRIMARY constituents are composed of the largest
percent of the soil mass. Primary constituents are
capitalized and bold (i.e., GRAVEL, SAND)
PRIMARY constituents are based on plasticity. Primary
constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e., CLAY, SILT)
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent. If the soil
mass consists of 12 percent or more fines content, a
cohesive constituent is used (SILTY or CLAYEY);
otherwise, a granular constituent is used (GRAVELLY
or SANDY) provided that the secondary constituent
consists of 20 percent or more of the soil mass.
Secondary constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e.,
SANDY GRAVEL, CLAYEY SAND) and precede the
primary constituent.
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent, but more
than 20 percent of the soil mass. Secondary constituents
are capitalized and bold (i.e., SANDY CLAY, SILTY
CLAY, CLAYEY SILT) and precede the primary
constituent.

APPENDIX B
W.O. 7859-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
DECEMBER 2019     Page B-1 
Hawaii • California 
A P P E N D I X   B 
Laboratory Tests 
Moisture Content (ASTM D2216) and Unit Weight determinations (ASTM D2937) 
were performed on selected soil samples as an aid in the classification and evaluation of 
soil properties. The test results are presented on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate 
sample depths. 
Eight Atterberg Limits tests (ASTM D4318) were performed on selected soil 
samples to evaluate the liquid and plastic limits. The test results are summarized on the 
Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. Graphic presentations of the test 
results are provided on Plate B-1. 
Four Sieve Analysis tests (ASTM C117 & C136) were performed on selected soil 
samples to evaluate the gradation characteristics of the soils and to aid in soil 
classification. Graphic presentations of the grain size distributions are provided on 
Plate B-2. 
Eight 
Unconsolidated-Undrained 
Triaxial 
Compression 
(TXUU) 
tests 
(ASTM D2850) were performed on selected soil samples to evaluate the undrained shear 
strength of the silty and clayey soils encountered. The approximate in-situ effective 
overburden pressure was used as the applied confining pressure for the relatively 
“undisturbed” soil sample. The test results and the stress-strain curves are presented on 
Plates B-3 through B-10. 
Four Direct Shear tests (ASTM D 3080) were performed on selected soil samples 
to evaluate the shear strength characteristics of the in-situ soils. The test results are 
presented on Plates B-11 through B-14. 
Four Modified Proctor compaction tests (ASTM D1557) were performed on bulk 
samples of the near-surface soils to evaluate the dry density and moisture content 
relationships.  The test results are presented on Plates B-15 through B-18. 
Four laboratory California Bearing Ratio tests (ASTM D1883) were performed on 
bulk samples of the near-surface soils to evaluate the pavement support characteristics 
of the soils. The test results are presented on Plates B-19 through B-22. 
Twenty-one sets of Corrosivity tests, including pH (ASTM G51), Minimum 
Resistivity (ASTM G57), Chloride Content (EPA 300.0) and Sulfate Content (EPA 300.0) 
tests were performed by our laboratory and TestAmerica Laboratories, Inc. on selected 
soil samples obtained from our field exploration.  Results of the corrosivity tests are 
presented on Plate B-23.

A P P E N D I X   C 
Field Permeability Tests 
The permeability of the near-surface materials at the project site was evaluated by 
performing constant and falling head permeability tests in Boring Nos. 4, 10 and 12, at the 
approximate locations shown on the Site Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.8. The tests were 
performed at depths of about 11.5 and 21.5 feet below the existing ground surface. 
During the constant head test, a water pump was used to pump clear water into the 
boring. The flow rate was recorded by timing and calculating the volume as a relatively 
constant flow rate was achieved. The results of the constant head permeability test are 
presented on Plate C-1. 
During the falling head test, the test hole was initially saturated. After saturation, 
the falling head percolation test was performed by filling up the test boring with clear 
water. Time intervals and water drops were then recorded to provide data upon which 
calculation of the permeability values were based. The results of the falling head 
permeability test are presented on Plates C-2 and C-3.
